<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Great American Interchange</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_American_Biotic_Interchange_examples.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="900" height="351" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Great_American_Biotic_Interchange_examples.svg/300px-Great_American_Biotic_Interchange_examples.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Great_American_Biotic_Interchange_examples.svg/450px-Great_American_Biotic_Interchange_examples.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Great_American_Biotic_Interchange_examples.svg/600px-Great_American_Biotic_Interchange_examples.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_American_Biotic_Interchange_examples.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Examples of migrant species in both Americas. Olive green silhouettes denote North American species with South American ancestors; blue silhouettes denote South American species of North American origin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>Great American Interchange</b> was an important late <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> <a href="/wiki/Zoogeography" title="Zoogeography">paleozoogeographic</a> event in which land and freshwater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fauna_(animals)" title="Fauna (animals)">fauna</a> migrated from North America via Central America to South America and vice versa, as the volcanic <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a> rose up from the sea floor and bridged the formerly separated <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>. The migration peaked dramatically around three million years (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ma_(unit)" title="Ma (unit)">Ma</a>) ago during the <a href="/wiki/Piacenzian" title="Piacenzian">Piacenzian</a> age. It resulted in the joining of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neotropic_ecozone" title="Neotropic ecozone">Neotropic</a> (roughly South America) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nearctic_ecozone" title="Nearctic ecozone">Nearctic</a> (roughly North America) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrestrial_ecozone" title="Terrestrial ecozone">ecozones</a> definitively to form the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. The interchange is visible from observation of both <a href="/wiki/Biostratigraphy" title="Biostratigraphy">biostratigraphy</a> and nature (<a href="/wiki/Neontology" title="Neontology">neontology</a>). Its most dramatic effect is on the <a href="/wiki/Zoogeography" title="Zoogeography">zoogeography</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> but it also gave an opportunity for <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, weak-flying or <a href="/wiki/Flightless_bird" title="Flightless bird">flightless birds</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_fish" title="Freshwater fish">freshwater fish</a> to migrate.</p>
<p>The occurrence of the interchange was first discussed in 1876 by the "father of <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeography</a>", <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace" title="Alfred Russel Wallace">Alfred Russel Wallace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WallaceVol.1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-WallaceVol.1-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WallaceVol.2_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-WallaceVol.2-2">[2]</a></sup> Wallace had spent 1848–1852 exploring and collecting specimens in the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_basin" title="Amazon basin">Amazon basin</a>. Others who made significant contributions to understanding the event in the century that followed include <a href="/wiki/Florentino_Ameghino" title="Florentino Ameghino">Florentino Ameghino</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Diller_Matthew" title="William Diller Matthew">W. D. Matthew</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Berryman_Scott" title="William Berryman Scott">W. B. Scott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Patterson" title="Bryan Patterson">Bryan Patterson</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Gaylord_Simpson" title="George Gaylord Simpson">George Gaylord Simpson</a> and S. David Webb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LandMammals_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-LandMammals-3">[3]</a></sup> The Pliocene timing of the formation of the connection between North and South America was discussed in 1910 by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fairfield_Osborn" title="Henry Fairfield Osborn">Henry Fairfield Osborn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Analogous interchanges occurred earlier in the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a>, when the formerly isolated land masses of <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">India</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and Africa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">made contact</a> with <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> c. 50 and 30 Ma ago, respectively.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span id="South_America's_endemic_fauna"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America.27s_endemic_fauna">South America's endemic fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_American_Interchange&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: South America's endemic fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thylacosmilus_Amerika.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="506" data-file-width="750" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Thylacosmilus_Amerika.jpg/220px-Thylacosmilus_Amerika.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Thylacosmilus_Amerika.jpg/330px-Thylacosmilus_Amerika.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Thylacosmilus_Amerika.jpg/440px-Thylacosmilus_Amerika.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thylacosmilus_Amerika.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Saber-toothed_cat" title="Saber-toothed cat">Sabertoothed</a> <a href="/wiki/Sparassodonta" title="Sparassodonta">sparassodont</a> †<i><a href="/wiki/Thylacosmilus" title="Thylacosmilus">Thylacosmilus</a></i> (with †<i><a href="/wiki/Glyptodon" title="Glyptodon">Glyptodon</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Toxodon" title="Toxodon">toxodonts</a> in the background)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Patagonia_and_Necrolestes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="960" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Patagonia_and_Necrolestes.jpg/220px-Patagonia_and_Necrolestes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Patagonia_and_Necrolestes.jpg/330px-Patagonia_and_Necrolestes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Patagonia_and_Necrolestes.jpg/440px-Patagonia_and_Necrolestes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Patagonia_and_Necrolestes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Miocene fauna of South America included some of the youngest non-therian mammals, †<i><a href="/wiki/Necrolestes" title="Necrolestes">Necrolestes</a></i> (right) and †<i><a href="/wiki/Patagonia_(mammal)" title="Patagonia (mammal)">Patagonia</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the late <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> breakup of <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>, South America spent most of the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> era as an island continent whose "splendid isolation" allowed its fauna to evolve into many forms found nowhere else on Earth, most of which are now <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GGSimpson_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-GGSimpson-7">[7]</a></sup> Its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic_(ecology)" title="Endemic (ecology)">endemic</a> mammals initially consisted primarily of <a href="/wiki/Metatheria" title="Metatheria">metatherians</a> (<a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">marsupials</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparassodonta" title="Sparassodonta">sparassodonts</a>), <a href="/wiki/Xenarthra" title="Xenarthra">xenarthrans</a>, and a diverse group of <a href="/wiki/Meridiungulata" title="Meridiungulata">native ungulates</a>: <a href="/wiki/Notoungulata" title="Notoungulata">notoungulates</a> (the "southern <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Litopterna" title="Litopterna">litopterns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astrapotheria" title="Astrapotheria">astrapotheres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyrotheria" title="Pyrotheria">pyrotheres</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenungulata" title="Xenungulata">xenungulates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[n 1]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[n 2]</a></sup> A few non-<a href="/wiki/Theria" title="Theria">therian</a> mammals – <a href="/wiki/Monotreme" title="Monotreme">monotremes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gondwanathere" title="Gondwanathere">gondwanatheres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dryolestida" title="Dryolestida">dryolestids</a> and possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cimolodont" title="Cimolodont">cimolodont</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multituberculate" title="Multituberculate">multituberculates</a> – were also present in the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a>; while none of these diversified significantly and most lineages did not survive long, forms like <i><a href="/wiki/Necrolestes" title="Necrolestes">Necrolestes</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Patagonia_(mammal)" title="Patagonia (mammal)">Patagonia</a></i> remained as recently as the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monito_del_Monte_ps6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="653" data-file-width="600" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Monito_del_Monte_ps6.jpg/180px-Monito_del_Monte_ps6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Monito_del_Monte_ps6.jpg/270px-Monito_del_Monte_ps6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Monito_del_Monte_ps6.jpg/360px-Monito_del_Monte_ps6.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monito_del_Monte_ps6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Monito_del_monte" title="Monito del monte">monito del monte</a>, <i>Dromiciops gliroides</i>, South America's only <a href="/wiki/Australidelphia" title="Australidelphia">australidelphian</a> marsupial</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AstrapotheriumDB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="753" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/AstrapotheriumDB.jpg/220px-AstrapotheriumDB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/AstrapotheriumDB.jpg/330px-AstrapotheriumDB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/AstrapotheriumDB.jpg/440px-AstrapotheriumDB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AstrapotheriumDB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
†<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrapotherium_magnum" title="Astrapotherium magnum">Astrapotherium magnum</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marsupials appear to have traveled via <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwanan</a> land connections from South America through <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> to <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a> in the late <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> or early <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">Tertiary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nilsson_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nilsson-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[n 3]</a></sup> One living South American marsupial, the <a href="/wiki/Monito_del_monte" title="Monito del monte">monito del monte</a>, has been shown to be more closely related to <a href="/wiki/Australidelphia" title="Australidelphia">Australian marsupials</a> than to <a href="/wiki/Ameridelphia" title="Ameridelphia">other South American marsupials</a>; however, it is the most <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> australidelphian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[n 4]</a></sup> meaning that this superorder arose in South America and then <a href="/wiki/Biological_dispersal" title="Biological dispersal">colonized</a> Australia after the monito del monte split off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nilsson_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nilsson-17">[15]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Obdurodon#Monotrematum_sudamericanum" title="Obdurodon">61-Ma-old platypus-like</a> monotreme fossil from <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> may represent an Australian immigrant. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleognath" title="Paleognath">Paleognath</a> birds (<a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a> and South American <a href="/wiki/Tinamou" title="Tinamou">tinamous</a>) may have migrated by this route around the same time, more likely in the direction from South America to Australia/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zealandia_(continent)" title="Zealandia (continent)">New Zealand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs-20">[16]</a></sup> Other taxa that may have dispersed by the same route (if not by flying or <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_dispersal" title="Oceanic dispersal">floating across the ocean</a>) are <a href="/wiki/Parrot" title="Parrot">parrots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelidae" title="Chelidae">chelid</a> turtles and (extinct) <a href="/wiki/Meiolaniidae" title="Meiolaniidae">meiolaniid</a> turtles.</p>
<p>Marsupials present in South America included didelphimorphs (<a href="/wiki/Opossum" title="Opossum">opossums</a>) and several other <a href="/wiki/Shrew_opossum" title="Shrew opossum">small</a> <a href="/wiki/Microbiotheria" title="Microbiotheria">groups</a>; larger predatory relatives of these also existed, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borhyaenid" title="Borhyaenid">borhyaenids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saber-toothed_cat" title="Saber-toothed cat">sabertooth</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Thylacosmilus" title="Thylacosmilus">Thylacosmilus</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Sparassodonta" title="Sparassodonta">sparassodont</a> <a href="/wiki/Metatheria" title="Metatheria">metatherians</a> which are no longer considered to be true marsupials).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup> After the extinction of sparassodonts, and before the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivorans</a>, giant opossums like <i><a href="/wiki/Thylophorops" title="Thylophorops">Thylophorops</a></i> represented true marsupial macropredators.</p>
<p>Metatherians (and a few xenarthran armadillos like <i><a href="/wiki/Macroeuphractus" title="Macroeuphractus">Macroeuphractus</a></i>) were the only South American mammals to specialize as <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivores</a>; their relative inefficiency created openings for nonmammalian predators to play more prominent roles than usual (similar to the <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna#Extinct_Australian_megafauna:_pre-1788" title="Australian megafauna">situation in Australia</a>). Sparassodonts and giant opossums shared the <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a> for large predators with fearsome flightless "terror birds" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phorusrhacid" title="Phorusrhacid">phorusrhacids</a>), whose closest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> relatives are the <a href="/wiki/Seriema" title="Seriema">seriemas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naish_blog_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naish_blog-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarenga_and_Hofling_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarenga_and_Hofling-23">[19]</a></sup> (Similar large terrestrial predatory birds, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathornithid" title="Bathornithid">bathornithids</a>, were found in North America during the early Cenozoic, but they died out in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Miocene" title="Early Miocene">Early Miocene</a>, about 20 million years ago.) Through the skies over late Miocene South America (6 Ma ago) soared the largest flying bird known, the <a href="/wiki/Teratornithidae" title="Teratornithidae">teratorn</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argentavis_magnificens" title="Argentavis magnificens"><i>Argentavis</i></a>, with a wing span of 6 m or more, which may have subsisted in part on the leftovers of <i>Thylacosmilus</i> kills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup> Terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Quinkana" title="Quinkana">ziphodont</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[n 5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sebecidae" title="Sebecidae">sebecid</a> <a href="/wiki/Crocodylomorpha" title="Crocodylomorpha">crocodilians</a> were also present at least through the middle Miocene<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasparini_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gasparini-29">[24]</a></sup> and maybe to the Miocene-Pliocene boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liccardo2010_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liccardo2010-30">[25]</a></sup> Some of South America's aquatic crocodilians, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Gryposuchus" title="Gryposuchus">Gryposuchus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mourasuchus" title="Mourasuchus">Mourasuchus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Purussaurus" title="Purussaurus">Purussaurus</a></i>, reached monstrous sizes, with lengths up to 12 m (comparable to the largest Mesozoic <a href="/wiki/Crocodyliformes" title="Crocodyliformes">crocodyliforms</a>). They shared their habitat with one of the largest turtles of all time, the 3.3 m (11 ft) <i><a href="/wiki/Stupendemys" title="Stupendemys">Stupendemys</a></i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NationalZooAnteater.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/NationalZooAnteater.JPG/220px-NationalZooAnteater.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/NationalZooAnteater.JPG/330px-NationalZooAnteater.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/NationalZooAnteater.JPG/440px-NationalZooAnteater.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NationalZooAnteater.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Giant_anteater" title="Giant anteater">giant anteater</a>, <i>Myrmecophaga tridactyla</i>, the largest living descendant of South American's early <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> mammalian fauna</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Xenarthra" title="Xenarthra">Xenarthrans</a> are a curious group of mammals that developed morphological adaptations for specialized diets very early in their history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M.C3.B6ller-Krull_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-M.C3.B6ller-Krull-31">[26]</a></sup> In addition to those extant today (<a href="/wiki/Armadillo" title="Armadillo">armadillos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anteater" title="Anteater">anteaters</a> and tree <a href="/wiki/Sloth" title="Sloth">sloths</a>), a great diversity of larger types were present, including <a href="/wiki/Pampatheriidae" title="Pampatheriidae">pampatheres</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ankylosauridae" title="Ankylosauridae">ankylosaur</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Glyptodont" title="Glyptodont">glyptodonts</a>, predatory <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euphractine" title="Euphractine">euphractines</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Ground_sloth" title="Ground sloth">ground sloths</a>, some of which reached the size of elephants (e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Megatherium" title="Megatherium">Megatherium</a></i>), and even semiaquatic to aquatic <a href="/wiki/Thalassocnus" title="Thalassocnus">marine sloths</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muizen2004_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muizen2004-32">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amsonetal2014_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amsonetal2014-33">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Macrauchenia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="1091" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Macrauchenia.jpg/220px-Macrauchenia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Macrauchenia.jpg/330px-Macrauchenia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Macrauchenia.jpg/440px-Macrauchenia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Macrauchenia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Litopterna" title="Litopterna">litoptern</a> <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a><i><a href="/wiki/Macrauchenia" title="Macrauchenia">Macrauchenia</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The notoungulates and litopterns had many strange forms, like <i><a href="/wiki/Macrauchenia" title="Macrauchenia">Macrauchenia</a></i>, a camel-like litoptern with a small <a href="/wiki/Proboscis" title="Proboscis">proboscis</a>. They also produced a number of familiar-looking body types that represent examples of <a href="/wiki/Parallel_evolution" title="Parallel evolution">parallel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>: one-toed <i><a href="/wiki/Thoatherium" title="Thoatherium">Thoatherium</a></i> had legs like those of a horse, <i><a href="/wiki/Pachyrukhos" title="Pachyrukhos">Pachyrukhos</a></i> resembled a rabbit, <i><a href="/wiki/Homalodotherium" title="Homalodotherium">Homalodotherium</a></i> was a semi-bipedal clawed browser like a <a href="/wiki/Chalicothere" title="Chalicothere">chalicothere</a>, and horned <i><a href="/wiki/Trigodon" title="Trigodon">Trigodon</a></i> looked like a <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">rhino</a>. Both groups started evolving in the Lower Paleocene, possibly from <a href="/wiki/Condylarth" title="Condylarth">condylarth</a> stock, diversified, dwindled before the great interchange, and went <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>. The pyrotheres and astrapotheres were also strange but were less diverse and disappeared earlier, well before the interchange.</p>
<p>The North American fauna was a typical <a href="/wiki/Boreoeutheria" title="Boreoeutheria">boreoeutherian</a> one (supplemented with <a href="/wiki/Afrotheria" title="Afrotheria">Afrotherian</a> <a href="/wiki/Proboscidea" title="Proboscidea">proboscids</a>).</p>
<h2><span id="Island-hopping_'waif_dispersers'"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Island-hopping_.27waif_dispersers.27">Island-hopping 'waif dispersers'</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_American_Interchange&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Island-hopping 'waif dispersers'">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Capibara_2_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1300" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Capibara_2_edit.jpg/220px-Capibara_2_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Capibara_2_edit.jpg/330px-Capibara_2_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Capibara_2_edit.jpg/440px-Capibara_2_edit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Capibara_2_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Capybara" title="Capybara">Capybara</a>, <i>Hydrochoerus hydrochaeris</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tamarin_portrait_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="1944" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Tamarin_portrait_edit.jpg/220px-Tamarin_portrait_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Tamarin_portrait_edit.jpg/330px-Tamarin_portrait_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Tamarin_portrait_edit.jpg/440px-Tamarin_portrait_edit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tamarin_portrait_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Emperor_tamarin" title="Emperor tamarin">Emperor tamarin</a>, <i>Saguinus imperator</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The invasions of South America started about 40 Ma ago (middle <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>), when <a href="/wiki/Caviomorpha" title="Caviomorpha">caviomorph rodents</a> arrived in South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poux_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poux-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mangels_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mangels-35">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AntoineMarivaux2011_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-AntoineMarivaux2011-36">[31]</a></sup> Their subsequent vigorous <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">diversification</a> displaced some of South America's small marsupials and gave rise to – among others – <a href="/wiki/Capybara" title="Capybara">capybaras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinchilla" title="Chinchilla">chinchillas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viscacha" title="Viscacha">viscachas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_World_porcupine" title="New World porcupine">New World porcupines</a>. (The independent development of <a href="/wiki/Spine_(zoology)" title="Spine (zoology)">spines</a> by New and <a href="/wiki/Old_World_porcupine" title="Old World porcupine">Old World porcupines</a> is another example of parallel evolution.) This invasion most likely came from Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flynn_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flynn-37">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciAm_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm-38">[33]</a></sup> The crossing from West Africa to the northeast corner of Brazil was much shorter then, due to <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a>, and may have been aided by <a href="/wiki/Island_hopping" title="Island hopping">island hopping</a> (e.g. via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_Rocks" title="St. Paul's Rocks">St. Paul's Rocks</a>, if they were an inhabitable island at the time) and westward oceanic currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fratantoni_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fratantoni-39">[34]</a></sup> Crossings of the ocean were accomplished when at least one fertilised female (more commonly a group of animals) accidentally <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_dispersal" title="Oceanic dispersal">floated over</a> on <a href="/wiki/Driftwood" title="Driftwood">driftwood</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangrove</a> rafts. (<a href="/wiki/Hutia" title="Hutia">Island-hopping caviomorphs</a> would subsequently colonize the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">West Indies</a> as far as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bahamas" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedges1996_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hedges1996-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedges2006_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hedges2006-41">[36]</a></sup> reaching the Greater Antilles by the early Oligocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V.C3.A9lez-Juarbe2014_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-V.C3.A9lez-Juarbe2014-42">[37]</a></sup>) Over time, some caviomorph rodents evolved into larger forms that competed with some of the native South American ungulates, which may have contributed to the gradual loss of diversity suffered by the latter after the early Oligocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GGSimpson_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-GGSimpson-7">[7]</a></sup> By the Pliocene, some caviomorphs (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Josephoartigasia_monesi" title="Josephoartigasia monesi">Josephoartigasia</a></i>) attained sizes on the order of 500 kg (1,100 lb) or larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millien_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millien-43">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later (by 36 Ma ago)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bond2015_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bond2015-44">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> followed, again from Africa in a fashion similar to that of the rodents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poux_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Poux-34">[29]</a></sup> Primates capable of migrating had to be small. Like caviomorph rodents, South American monkeys are believed to be a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a>). However, although they would have had little effective competition, all extant <a href="/wiki/New_World_monkey" title="New World monkey">New World monkeys</a> appear to derive from a radiation that occurred long afterwards, in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Miocene" title="Early Miocene">Early Miocene</a> about 18 Ma ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poux_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Poux-34">[29]</a></sup> Subsequent to this, monkeys apparently most closely related to <a href="/wiki/Titi" title="Titi">titis</a> island-hopped to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban_monkey" title="Cuban monkey">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola_monkey" title="Hispaniola monkey">Hispaniola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_monkey" title="Jamaican monkey">Jamaica</a>. Additionally, a find of seven 21-Ma-old apparent cebid teeth in Panama suggests that South American monkeys had dispersed across the seaway separating Central and South America by that early date. However, all extant Central American monkeys are believed to be descended from much later migrants, and there is as yet no evidence that these early Central American cebids established an extensive or long-lasting population, perhaps due to a shortage of suitable rainforest habitat at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wade2016_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wade2016-45">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloch2016_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloch2016-46">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Remarkably, the descendents of those few bedraggled <a href="/wiki/Waif" title="Waif">waifs</a> that crawled ashore from their rafts of African flotsam in the Eocene now constitute more than twice as many of South America's species as the descendents of all the nonflying mammals previously resident on the continent (<a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_mammals" title="List of South American mammals">372 caviomorph and monkey species versus 136 marsupial and xenarthran species</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[n 6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many of South America's bats may have arrived from Africa during roughly the same period, possibly with the aid of intervening islands, although by flying rather than floating. Noctilionoid bats ancestral to those in the neotropical families <a href="/wiki/Furipteridae" title="Furipteridae">Furipteridae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mormoopidae" title="Mormoopidae">Mormoopidae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noctilionidae" title="Noctilionidae">Noctilionidae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyllostomidae" title="Phyllostomidae">Phyllostomidae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thyropteridae" title="Thyropteridae">Thyropteridae</a> are thought to have reached South America from Africa in the Eocene,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lim2009_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lim2009-49">[43]</a></sup> possibly via Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gunnell2014_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gunnell2014-50">[44]</a></sup> Similarly, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molossidae" title="Molossidae">molossid</a> bats may have reached South America from Africa in as many as five dispersals, starting in the Eocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lim2009_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lim2009-49">[43]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emballonuridae" title="Emballonuridae">Emballonurid bats</a> may have also reached South America from Africa about 30 Ma ago, based on molecular evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lim2009_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lim2009-49">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teeling2005_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teeling2005-51">[45]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vespertilionidae" title="Vespertilionidae">Vespertilionid</a> bats may have arrived in five dispersals from North America and one from Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lim2009_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lim2009-49">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Natalidae" title="Natalidae">Natalid</a> bats are thought to have arrived during the <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> from North America via the Caribbean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lim2009_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lim2009-49">[43]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geochelone_carbonaria_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Geochelone_carbonaria_1.jpg/220px-Geochelone_carbonaria_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Geochelone_carbonaria_1.jpg/330px-Geochelone_carbonaria_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Geochelone_carbonaria_1.jpg/440px-Geochelone_carbonaria_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geochelone_carbonaria_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Red-footed_tortoise" title="Red-footed tortoise">Red-footed tortoise</a>, <i>Chelonoidis carbonaria</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tortoises also arrived in South America in the Oligocene. It was long thought that they had come from North America, but a recent comparative genetic analysis concludes that the South American genus <i><a href="/wiki/Chelonoidis" title="Chelonoidis">Chelonoidis</a></i> (formerly part of <i><a href="/wiki/Geochelone" title="Geochelone">Geochelone</a></i>) is actually most closely related to African <a href="/wiki/Kinixys" title="Kinixys">hingeback tortoises</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[n 7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Le_et_al._53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Le_et_al.-53">[46]</a></sup> Tortoises are aided in oceanic dispersal by their ability to float with their heads up, and to survive up to six months without food or water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Le_et_al._53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Le_et_al.-53">[46]</a></sup> South American tortoises then went on to colonize the West Indies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[47]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_tortoise" title="Galápagos tortoise">Galápagos Islands</a>. A number of clades of American <a href="/wiki/Gecko" title="Gecko">geckos</a> seem to have rafted over from Africa during both the Paleogene and Neogene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gamble2010_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gamble2010-55">[48]</a></sup> Skinks of the related genera <i><a href="/wiki/Mabuya" title="Mabuya">Mabuya</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Trachylepis" title="Trachylepis">Trachylepis</a></i> apparently dispersed across the Atlantic from Africa to South America and <a href="/wiki/Fernando_de_Noronha" title="Fernando de Noronha">Fernando de Noronha</a>, respectively, during the last 9 Ma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[49]</a></sup> Surprisingly, South America's burrowing <a href="/wiki/Amphisbaenia" title="Amphisbaenia">amphisbaenians</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vidal2007_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vidal2007-57">[50]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blind_snake" title="Blind snake">blind snakes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vidal2010_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vidal2010-58">[51]</a></sup> also appear to have rafted from Africa, as does the <a href="/wiki/Hoatzin" title="Hoatzin">hoatzin</a>, a weak-flying bird of South American rainforests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayr2011_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayr2011-59">[52]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Megalonyx_wheatleyi.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Megalonyx_wheatleyi.JPG/220px-Megalonyx_wheatleyi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Megalonyx_wheatleyi.JPG/330px-Megalonyx_wheatleyi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Megalonyx_wheatleyi.JPG/440px-Megalonyx_wheatleyi.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Megalonyx_wheatleyi.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
†<a href="/wiki/Megalonyx" title="Megalonyx"><i>Megalonyx wheatleyi</i></a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The earliest traditionally recognized mammalian arrival from North America was a <a href="/wiki/Procyonidae" title="Procyonidae">procyonid</a> that island-hopped from Central America before the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">isthmus of Panama</a> <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridge</a> formed, around 7.3 Ma ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodburne2010_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woodburne2010-60">[53]</a></sup> This was South America's first <a href="/wiki/Eutheria" title="Eutheria">eutherian</a> carnivore. South American procyonids then diversified into forms now extinct (e.g. the "dog-coati" <i><a href="/wiki/Cyonasua" title="Cyonasua">Cyonasua</a></i>, which evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bear</a>-like <i><a href="/wiki/Chapalmalania" title="Chapalmalania">Chapalmalania</a></i>). However, all extant procyonid genera appear to have originated in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[54]</a></sup> It has been suggested that the first South American procyonids may have contributed to the extinction of sebecid crocodilians by eating their eggs, but this view has not been universally viewed as plausible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[n 8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasparini_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gasparini-29">[24]</a></sup> The procyonids were followed to South America by rafting/island-hopping <a href="/wiki/Hog-nosed_skunk" title="Hog-nosed skunk">hog-nosed skunks</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SDWebb_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-SDWebb-63">[55]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sigmodontinae" title="Sigmodontinae">sigmodontine rodents</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LGMarshall_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-LGMarshall-64">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engel_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engel-65">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithMF_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-SmithMF-66">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parada2013_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parada2013-67">[59]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Oryzomyini" title="Oryzomyini">oryzomyine</a> tribe of sigmodontine rodents went on to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodents_of_the_Caribbean#Overview" title="Rodents of the Caribbean">colonize</a> the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Antilles" title="Lesser Antilles">Lesser Antilles</a> up to <a href="/wiki/Anguilla" title="Anguilla">Anguilla</a>.</p>
<p>One group has proposed that a number of large Neartic herbivores actually reached South America as early as 9–10 Ma ago, in the late Miocene, via the "Baudo pathway", an early land bridge that was probably incomplete and required some swimming and island-hopping to traverse. The limited evidence for these early immigrants may reflect their presence primarily in the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_basin" title="Amazon basin">Amazon basin</a>, an area where fewer fossils have been collected. These taxa are: a proboscidean (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Amahuacatherium&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Amahuacatherium (page does not exist)">Amahuacatherium</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2000_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2000-68">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2009_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2009-69">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[n 9]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peccaries" title="Peccaries">peccaries</a> (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sylvochoerus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sylvochoerus (page does not exist)">Sylvochoerus</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Waldochoerus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Waldochoerus (page does not exist)">Waldochoerus</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frailey2012_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frailey2012-72">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tapir" title="Tapir">tapirs</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeomerycid" title="Palaeomerycid">palaeomerycid</a> (from a family probably ancestral to cervids), <i><a href="/wiki/Surameryx" title="Surameryx">Surameryx</a></i>; the paleomerycids were probably unable to successfully colonize South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero2014_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero2014-73">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly, megalonychid and mylodontid <a href="/wiki/Ground_sloth" title="Ground sloth">ground sloths</a> island-hopped to North America by 9 Ma ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LGMarshall_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-LGMarshall-64">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pilosans_of_the_Caribbean" title="Pilosans of the Caribbean">Megalonychids</a> had colonized the <a href="/wiki/Antilles" title="Antilles">Antilles</a> previously, by the early <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-74">[65]</a></sup> (Megatheriid and nothrotheriid ground sloths did not migrate north until the formation of the isthmus.) <a href="/wiki/Phorusrhacidae" title="Phorusrhacidae">Terror birds</a> may have also island-hopped to North America as early as 5 Ma ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcfaddenetal2007_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcfaddenetal2007-75">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Caribbean islands were populated primarily by species from South America. This was due to the prevailing direction of oceanic currents, rather than to a competition between North and South American forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedges1996_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hedges1996-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedges2006_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hedges2006-41">[36]</a></sup> (Except in the case of <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, oryzomyine rodents of North American origin were able to enter the region only after invading South America.)</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Great_American_Biotic_Interchange">The Great American Biotic Interchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_American_Interchange&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The Great American Biotic Interchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opossum_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2202" data-file-width="3400" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Opossum_1.jpg/220px-Opossum_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Opossum_1.jpg/330px-Opossum_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Opossum_1.jpg/440px-Opossum_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opossum_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Virginia_opossum" title="Virginia opossum">Virginia opossum</a>, <i>Didelphis virginiana</i>, the only marsupial in temperate North America</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The formation of the Isthmus of Panama led to the last and most conspicuous wave, the great interchange, around 3 Ma ago. This included the immigration into South America of North American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ungulates" title="Ungulates">ungulates</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Camelid" title="Camelid">camelids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tapir" title="Tapir">tapirs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a>), <a href="/wiki/Proboscidea" title="Proboscidea">proboscids</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gomphothere" title="Gomphothere">gomphotheres</a>), <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivorans</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">felids</a> like <a href="/wiki/Cougar" title="Cougar">cougars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machairodontinae" title="Machairodontinae">saber-toothed cats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canidae" title="Canidae">canids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustelidae" title="Mustelidae">mustelids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Procyonidae" title="Procyonidae">procyonids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a>) and a number of types of <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[n 10]</a></sup>. The larger members of the reverse migration, besides ground sloths and terror birds, were <a href="/wiki/Glyptodont" title="Glyptodont">glyptodonts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pampatheriidae" title="Pampatheriidae">pampatheres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neochoerus_pinckneyi" title="Neochoerus pinckneyi">capybaras</a> and the notoungulate <i><a href="/wiki/Mixotoxodon" title="Mixotoxodon">Mixotoxodon</a></i> (the only South American ungulate known to have invaded Central America).</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctotherium.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="642" data-file-width="791" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Arctotherium.jpg/220px-Arctotherium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Arctotherium.jpg/330px-Arctotherium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Arctotherium.jpg/440px-Arctotherium.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctotherium.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
†<i><a href="/wiki/Arctotherium" title="Arctotherium">Arctotherium</a> bonariense</i>, a South American short-faced bear</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In general, the initial net migration was symmetrical. Later on, however, the Neotropic species proved far less successful than the Nearctic. This misfortune happened both ways. Northwardly migrating animals often were not able to compete for resources as well as the North American species already occupying the same ecological niches; those that did become established were not able to diversify much.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GAIpatterns_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-GAIpatterns-77">[67]</a></sup> Southwardly migrating Nearctic species established themselves in larger numbers and diversified considerably more,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GAIpatterns_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-GAIpatterns-77">[67]</a></sup> and are thought to have caused the <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> of a large proportion of the South American fauna. (There were no extinctions in North America plainly linked to South American immigrants.) Although terror birds were able to invade part of North America, their success was temporary; this lineage disappeared about two million years ago. The large Neotropic metatherian predators fared no better. <a href="/wiki/Meridiungulata" title="Meridiungulata">Native South American ungulates</a> also did poorly, with only a handful of genera withstanding the northern onslaught. (It has long been recognized that several of the largest forms, <a href="/wiki/Macraucheniidae" title="Macraucheniidae">macraucheniids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toxodontidae" title="Toxodontidae">toxodontids</a>, survived to the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>. Recent fossil finds indicate that one species of the horse-like <a href="/wiki/Proterotheriidae" title="Proterotheriidae">proterotheriid</a> litopterns did as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaudioso2017_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaudioso2017-78">[68]</a></sup> The notoungulate <a href="/wiki/Mesotheriidae" title="Mesotheriidae">mesotheriids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hegetotheriidae" title="Hegetotheriidae">hegetotheriids</a> also managed to hold on at least part way through the Pleistocene.)<sup class="reference" id="ref_Anone"><a href="#endnote_Anone">[A]</a></sup> On the other hand, South America's <a href="/wiki/Ameridelphia" title="Ameridelphia">small marsupials</a> survived in large numbers, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primitive_(biology)" title="Primitive (biology)">primitive</a>-looking <a href="/wiki/Xenarthra" title="Xenarthra">xenarthrans</a> proved to be surprisingly competitive and became the most successful invaders of North America. The African immigrants, the caviomorph rodents and platyrrhine monkeys, were less impacted by the interchange than most of South America's 'old-timers', although the caviomorphs suffered a significant loss of diversity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[n 11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[n 12]</a></sup> including the elimination of the largest forms (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Dinomyidae" title="Dinomyidae">dinomyids</a>). With the exception of the <a href="/wiki/North_American_porcupine" title="North American porcupine">North American porcupine</a> and several extinct porcupines and capybaras, however, they did not migrate past Central America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[n 13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phorusrhacid_skeleton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="663" data-file-width="839" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Phorusrhacid_skeleton.jpg/220px-Phorusrhacid_skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Phorusrhacid_skeleton.jpg/330px-Phorusrhacid_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Phorusrhacid_skeleton.jpg/440px-Phorusrhacid_skeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phorusrhacid_skeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
†<i><a href="/wiki/Titanis" title="Titanis">Titanis walleri</a></i>, the only known North American <a href="/wiki/Phorusrhacidae" title="Phorusrhacidae">terror bird</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The initial wave of southwardly migrating Nearctic carnivorans rapidly occupied the South American predatory niches, displacing phorusrhacids and sparassodonts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[n 14]</a></sup> as well as eliminating <i>Chapalmalania</i>. It has been argued that canids probably played the major role in the borhyaenids' extinction; they are ecologically and morphologically more similar to them than other carnivorans, and are also the most diverse family of modern carnivorans on the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WangTedford2010_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-WangTedford2010-85">[71]</a></sup> The paucity of early competition and plentiful prey seems to have allowed short-faced bears to rapidly evolve into the largest known bear or terrestrial mammalian carnivore species; <i><a href="/wiki/Arctotherium" title="Arctotherium">Arctotherium</a> angustidens</i> is estimated to have weighed around 1600 kg. Later species of <i>Arctotherium</i> exhibited a trend towards smaller size and a more omnivorous diet, probably due to increasing competition from later-arriving or evolving carnivores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodge_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hodge-86">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soibelzon2_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soibelzon2-87">[73]</a></sup> In contrast, <i><a href="/wiki/Smilodon" title="Smilodon">Smilodon</a></i> showed a trend toward increasing body size that culminated in the appearance of <i><a href="/wiki/Smilodon#Classification_and_species" title="Smilodon">S. populator</a></i>, at up to nearly 500 kg the most massive felid known.</p>
<p>Due in large part to the success of the xenarthrans, one area of South American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecospace" title="Ecospace">ecospace</a> the Nearctic invaders were unable to dominate was the niches for megaherbivores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cione_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cione-88">[74]</a></sup> Before 12,000 years ago, South America was home to about 25 species of herbivores weighing more than 1000 kg, consisting of Neotropic ground sloths, glyptodonts and toxodontids, as well as gomphotheres and camelids of Nearctic origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[n 15]</a></sup> Native South American forms made up about 75% of these species. However, none of these megaherbivores have survived.</p>
<p>Armadillos, opossums and porcupines are present in North America today because of the Great American Interchange. Opossums and porcupines were among most successful northward migrants, reaching as far as Canada and <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, respectively. Most major groups of xenarthrans were present in North America up until the end-<a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a> (as a result of at least eight successful invasions of temperate North America, and at least six more invasions of Central America only). Among the <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a>, ground sloths were notably successful emigrants; <i><a href="/wiki/Megalonyx" title="Megalonyx">Megalonyx</a></i> spread as far north as the <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[76]</a></sup> and Alaska,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[77]</a></sup> and might well have eventually reached Eurasia if the extinction event had not intervened.</p>
<p>Generally speaking, however, the dispersal and subsequent explosive <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">adaptive radiation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sigmodontinae" title="Sigmodontinae">sigmodontine rodents</a> throughout South America (leading to <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_mammals#Order:_Rodentia_.28rodents.29" title="List of South American mammals">over 80 currently recognized genera</a>) was vastly more successful (both spatially and by number of species) than any northward migration of South American mammals. Other examples of North American mammal groups that diversified conspicuously in South America include canids and cervids, both of which currently have 3 or 4 genera in North America, 2 or 3 in Central America, and 6 in South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canidlisting_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-canidlisting-93">[n 16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[n 17]</a></sup> Although members of <i><a href="/wiki/Canis" title="Canis">Canis</a></i> (specifically, <a href="/wiki/Coyote" title="Coyote">coyotes</a>) currently range only as far south as Panama,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[n 18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_mammals#Order:_Carnivora_.28carnivorans.29" title="List of South American mammals">South America still has more extant genera</a> of <a href="/wiki/Canidae" title="Canidae">canids</a> than any other continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canidlisting_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-canidlisting-93">[n 16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The effect of formation of the isthmus on the marine biota of the area was the inverse of its effect on terrestrial organisms, a development that has been termed the "Great American Schism". The connection between the east Pacific Ocean and the Caribbean (the <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Seaway" title="Central American Seaway">Central American Seaway</a>) was severed, setting now-separated populations on divergent evolutionary paths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lessios_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lessios-99">[81]</a></sup> Caribbean species also had to adapt to an environment of lower productivity after the inflow of <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">nutrient-rich water of deep Pacific origin</a> was blocked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jain_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jain-100">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reasons_for_success_or_failure">Reasons for success or failure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_American_Interchange&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Reasons for success or failure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Americas_Koppen_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1360" data-file-width="1096" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Americas_Koppen_Map.png/240px-Americas_Koppen_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Americas_Koppen_Map.png/360px-Americas_Koppen_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Americas_Koppen_Map.png/480px-Americas_Koppen_Map.png 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Americas_Koppen_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A north-south climatic asymmetry in the Americas. Tropical climate zones, which are warm year-round and moist at least part of the year (blue zones <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equatorial_climate" title="Equatorial climate">Af</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">Am</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_savanna_climate" title="Tropical savanna climate">Aw</a>), cover much of South America and nearly all of Central America, but very little of the rest of North America.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The eventual triumph of the Nearctic migrants was ultimately based on <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, which played into the hands of the northern invaders in two crucial respects. The first was a matter of <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>. Any species that reached <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> from either direction obviously had to be able to tolerate <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification#GROUP_A:_Tropical.2Fmegathermal_climates" title="Köppen climate classification">moist tropical conditions</a>. Those migrating southward would then be able to occupy much of South America without encountering climates that were markedly different. However, northward migrants would have encountered drier and/or cooler conditions by the time they reached the vicinity of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Mexican_Volcanic_Belt" title="Trans-Mexican Volcanic Belt">Trans-Mexican Volcanic Belt</a>. The challenge this climatic asymmetry (see map on right) presented was particularly acute for Neotropic species specialized for <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforest</a> environments, who had little prospect of penetrating beyond Central America. As a result, Central America currently has 41 mammal species of Neotropical origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAlisting_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAlisting-101">[n 19]</a></sup> compared to only 3 for temperate North America. However, species of South American origin (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Didelphidae" title="Didelphidae">marsupials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenarthra" title="Xenarthra">xenarthrans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caviomorpha" title="Caviomorpha">caviomorph rodents</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platyrrhini" title="Platyrrhini">monkeys</a>) still comprise only 21% of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Central_American_mammals" title="List of Central American mammals">species from nonflying, nonmarine mammal groups in Central America</a>, while North American invaders constitute <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_mammals" title="List of South American mammals">49% of species from such groups in South America</a>. Thus, climate alone cannot fully account for the greater success of species of Nearctic origin during the interchange.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nearctic-Neotropic_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1404" data-file-width="2759" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Nearctic-Neotropic_map.png/240px-Nearctic-Neotropic_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Nearctic-Neotropic_map.png/360px-Nearctic-Neotropic_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Nearctic-Neotropic_map.png/480px-Nearctic-Neotropic_map.png 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nearctic-Neotropic_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Land areas over which ancestors of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neotropic_ecozone" title="Neotropic ecozone">Neotropic</a> (green) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nearctic_ecozone" title="Nearctic ecozone">Nearctic</a> (red) species could wander via two-way migrations during the latter part of the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> prior to the interchange. The smaller area available for Neotropic species to evolve in tended to put them at a competitive disadvantage.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The second and more important advantage geography gave to the northerners is related to the land area available for their ancestors to evolve in. During the Cenozoic, North America was periodically connected to Eurasia via <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia</a>, allowing multiple migrations back and forth to unite the faunas of the two continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[n 20]</a></sup> Eurasia was <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">connected in turn to Africa</a>, which contributed further to the species that made their way to North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[n 21]</a></sup> South America, on the other hand, was connected only to Antarctica and Australia, two much smaller and less hospitable continents, and only in the early Cenozoic. Moreover, this land connection does not seem to have carried much traffic (apparently no mammals other than marsupials and perhaps a few monotremes ever migrated by this route), particularly in the direction of South America. This means that Northern Hemisphere species arose over a land area roughly six times greater than was available to South American species. North American species were thus products of a larger and more competitive arena,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[n 22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GAIpatterns_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-GAIpatterns-77">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panda_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Panda-105">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-106">[84]</a></sup> where <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> would have proceeded more rapidly. They tended to be more efficient and <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brainier</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[n 23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[n 24]</a></sup> generally able to outrun and outwit their South American counterparts, who were products of an evolutionary backwater. These advantages can be clearly seen in the cases of ungulates and their predators, where South American forms were replaced wholesale by the invaders.</p>
<p>The greater eventual success of South America's African immigrants compared to its native early Cenozoic mammal fauna is another example of this phenomenon, since the former evolved over a greater land area; their ancestors migrated from <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, two significantly larger continents, before finding their way to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaimanee_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chaimanee-47">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Against this backdrop, the ability of South America's <a href="/wiki/Xenarthra" title="Xenarthra">xenarthrans</a> to compete effectively against the northerners represents a special case. The explanation for the xenarthrans' success lies in part in their idiosyncratic approach to defending against <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a>, based on possession of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_shell" title="Animal shell">body armor</a> and/or formidable <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">claws</a>. The xenarthrans did not need to be fleet-footed or quick-witted to survive. Such a strategy may have been forced on them by their low <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">metabolic rate</a> (the lowest among the <a href="/wiki/Theria" title="Theria">therians</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovegrove_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lovegrove-117">[93]</a></sup> Their low metabolic rate may in turn have been advantageous in allowing them to subsist on less abundant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McNab_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-McNab-118">[94]</a></sup> and/or less nutritious food sources. Unfortunately, the defensive adaptations of the large xenarthrans would have offered little protection against humans armed with <a href="/wiki/Spear#Prehistory" title="Spear">spears</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Projectile#See_also" title="Projectile">projectiles</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Pleistocene_extinctions">Late Pleistocene extinctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_American_Interchange&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Late Pleistocene extinctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Porcupine_NPS11952.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1355" data-file-width="1915" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Porcupine_NPS11952.jpg/220px-Porcupine_NPS11952.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Porcupine_NPS11952.jpg/330px-Porcupine_NPS11952.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Porcupine_NPS11952.jpg/440px-Porcupine_NPS11952.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Porcupine_NPS11952.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/North_American_porcupine" title="North American porcupine">North American porcupine</a>, <i>Erethizon dorsatum</i>, the largest surviving Neotropic migrant to temperate North America</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene epoch</a>, about 12,000 years ago, three dramatic developments occurred in the Americas at roughly the same time (geologically speaking). <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Paleoindians</a> invaded and occupied the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> came to an end, and a large fraction of the <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> of both North and South America went extinct. This <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">wave of extinctions</a> swept off the face of the Earth many of the successful participants of the Great American Interchange, as well as other species that had not migrated. All the pampatheres, glyptodonts, ground sloths, equids, proboscids,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agenbroad_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agenbroad-119">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-120">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prado_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prado-121">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tremarctinae" title="Tremarctinae">giant short-faced bears</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dire_wolves" title="Dire wolves">dire wolves</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machairodont" title="Machairodont">machairodont</a> species of both continents disappeared. The last of the South and Central American notoungulates and litopterns died out, as well as North America's <a href="/wiki/Castoroides" title="Castoroides">giant beavers</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_lion" title="American lion">lions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhole" title="Dhole">dholes</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_cheetah" title="American cheetah">cheetahs</a>, and many of its <a href="/wiki/Capromeryx_minor" title="Capromeryx minor">antilocaprid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shrub-ox" title="Shrub-ox">bovid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stag-moose" title="Stag-moose">cervid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tapir" title="Tapir">tapirid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Platygonus" title="Platygonus">tayassuid</a> ungulates. Some groups disappeared over most or all of their original range but survived in their adopted homes, e.g. South American tapirs, camelids and tremarctine bears (cougars and jaguars may have been temporarily reduced to South American <a href="/wiki/Refugium_(population_biology)" title="Refugium (population biology)">refugia</a> also). Others, such as capybaras, survived in their original range but died out in areas they had migrated to. Notably, this extinction pulse eliminated all Neotropic migrants to North America larger than about 15 kg (the size of a big <a href="/wiki/North_American_porcupine" title="North American porcupine">porcupine</a>), and all native South American mammals larger than about 65 kg (the size of a big capybara or <a href="/wiki/Giant_anteater" title="Giant anteater">giant anteater</a>). In contrast, the largest surviving native North American mammal, the <a href="/wiki/Wood_bison" title="Wood bison">wood bison</a>, can exceed 900 kg, and the largest surviving Nearctic migrant to South America, <a href="/wiki/Baird%27s_tapir" title="Baird's tapir">Baird's tapir</a>, can reach 400 kg.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glyptodon_old_drawing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="412" data-file-width="653" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Glyptodon_old_drawing.jpg/220px-Glyptodon_old_drawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Glyptodon_old_drawing.jpg/330px-Glyptodon_old_drawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Glyptodon_old_drawing.jpg/440px-Glyptodon_old_drawing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glyptodon_old_drawing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Paleo-Indians</a> and †<i><a href="/wiki/Glyptodon" title="Glyptodon">Glyptodon</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tapirus_bairdii_-Franklin_Park_Zoo,_Massachusetts,_USA-8a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="3051" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Tapirus_bairdii_-Franklin_Park_Zoo%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA-8a.jpg/220px-Tapirus_bairdii_-Franklin_Park_Zoo%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA-8a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Tapirus_bairdii_-Franklin_Park_Zoo%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA-8a.jpg/330px-Tapirus_bairdii_-Franklin_Park_Zoo%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Tapirus_bairdii_-Franklin_Park_Zoo%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA-8a.jpg/440px-Tapirus_bairdii_-Franklin_Park_Zoo%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA-8a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tapirus_bairdii_-Franklin_Park_Zoo,_Massachusetts,_USA-8a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Baird%27s_tapir" title="Baird's tapir">Baird's tapir</a>, <i>Tapirus bairdii</i>, the largest surviving Nearctic migrant to South America</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The near-simultaneity of the megafaunal extinctions with the glacial retreat and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Models_of_migration_to_the_New_World" title="Models of migration to the New World">peopling of the Americas</a> has led to proposals that both climate change and human hunting played a role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cione_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cione-88">[74]</a></sup> Although the subject is contentious,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[102]</a></sup> a number of considerations suggest that human activities were pivotal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-89">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burney_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burney-127">[103]</a></sup> The extinctions did not occur selectively in the climatic zones that would have been most affected by the warming trend, and there is no plausible general climate-based megafauna-killing mechanism that could explain the continent-wide extinctions. The climate change took place worldwide, but had little effect on the megafauna in areas like Africa and southern Asia, where megafaunal species had coevolved with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_(genus)" title="Homo (genus)">humans</a>. Numerous <a href="/wiki/File:Five_Myr_Climate_Change.png" title="File:Five Myr Climate Change.png">very similar</a> <a href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_CO2_with_glaciers_cycles.gif" title="File:Atmospheric CO2 with glaciers cycles.gif">glacial retreats</a> had occurred previously within the <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a> of the last several Ma without ever producing comparable waves of extinction in the Americas or anywhere else. Similar megafaunal extinctions have occurred on other recently populated land masses (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Ages_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_Ages-128">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson2016_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson2016-129">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japan#Prehistory_and_ancient_history" title="Japan">Japan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norton_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norton-130">[106]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction_event#Madagascar" title="Holocene extinction event">Madagascar</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madagascar_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madagascar-131">[107]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction_event#New_Zealand" title="Holocene extinction event">New Zealand</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holdaway_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holdaway-132">[108]</a></sup> and many smaller islands around the world, such as <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Cyprus#Epipalaeolithic" title="Prehistoric Cyprus">Cyprus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simmons1_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons1-133">[109]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Crete" title="Prehistoric Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tilos#Neolithic" title="Tilos">Tilos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_of_New_Caledonia#Faunal_Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity of New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-134">[110]</a></sup>) at different times that correspond closely to the first arrival of humans at each location. These extinction pulses invariably swept rapidly over the full extent of a contiguous land mass, regardless of whether it was an island or a hemisphere-spanning set of connected continents.
